                .build()

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/ente.202401010')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="d07fd44edc3e45888928d056657c596d"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d07fd44edc3e45888928d056657c596d">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'b80bed25-9423-4d08-a330-e2b3b2538f8c'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

        (function() {
            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {
                var store_domain = 'https://onlinelibrary.wiley.com';
                var session_id = '2AD1E319F7DC03306B906ABD4452C8AF';
                var url = ('https:' == document.location.protocol ? 'https://' : 'http://')
                    + "beacon.riskified.com?shop=" + store_domain + "&sid=" + session_id;
                var s = document.createElement('script');
                s.type = 'text/javascript';
                s.async = true;
                s.src = url;
                var x = document.getElementsByTagName('script')[0];
                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);
            }
            if (window.attachEvent)
                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)
            else
                window.addEventListener('load', riskifiedBeaconLoad, false);
        })();

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d9353333" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>MoS<sub>2</sub> is the most promising anode material for secondary battery with its unique 2D layered structure. However, the application of MoS<sub>2</sub> is restricted by the poor electrical conductivity and sluggish ion diffusion. Herein, hollow nanotubes constructed with highly conductive 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and polypyrrole (PPy) nanotubes are fabricated and used as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries. Remarkably, these hollow nanotubes show a high lithium-specific capacity of 755.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> and excellent sodium-specific capacity of 503.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. The enhanced electrochemical performance can be attributed to the rational design of unique 1D and 2D composite structure. First, the highly conductive 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> 2D nanosheets and hollow 1D PPy nanotube can effectively promote the charge transfer kinetics. However, the increased interlayer spacing of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> rapidly improves the insertion/extraction process of metal ions, and the vertical growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on the surface of the PPy nanotubes also exposes more energy storage sites. This work provides a new idea for the preparation of MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite materials, and also proposes a reference for its application in the secondary battery.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202401010-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202401010-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The continuously increasing demand of human society has prompted the exploitation of new energy sources, such as wind energy, solar energy, tidal energy, and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0001_R_d9353325e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Eur. Phys. J. Plus">1</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0005_R_d9353325e356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Chem.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been rapidly developed in the field of industrial application for the advantages of high energy density and power density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0006_R_d9353325e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Renewable Sustainable Energy Rev.">6</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0009_R_d9353325e361" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Energy Chem.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, sodium ion, which has semblable chemical properties to lithium and more natural abundance, had prompted to further development in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0010_R_d9353325e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano">10</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0012_R_d9353325e366" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As is known to all, electrode materials play a decisive role in electrochemistry performance of batteries, but the traditional anode materials have large volume change and high cost. Therefore, it is particularly important to find suitable new high-performance anode materials. By contrast, the molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>), which has a graphite-like 2D layered structure, has attracted much attention because of its high theoretical capacity for LIBs (670 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0013_R_d9353325e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater.">13</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0016_R_d9353325e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> but the low inherent conductivity (2 × 10<sup>−4</sup> S cm<sup>−1</sup>) and the structural instability during electrochemical cycles hinder its practical applications.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0017_R_d9353325e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Mater. Sci. Technol.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>MoS<sub>2</sub> exhibits three phases, named 1T, 2H, and 3R.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0018_R_d9353325e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Catal. Commun.">18</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0021_R_d9353325e395" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> has metallic properties and high conductivity due to its distorted octahedral coordination; the 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub> is the most stable phase in nature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0021_R_d9353325e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">21</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e405" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In light of this, many great efforts have been investigated to enhance the conductivity and ion diffusion rate of MoS<sub>2</sub> by defect engineering, controlling interlayer spacing, heteroatom doping, constructing the composite materials with other high conductivity materials (such as graphene, carbon nanotubes), and so on.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0032_R_d9353325e412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, these methods still lead to harsh fabrication conditions and hard to achieve multiple effects, which limit mass production. So far, it necessary to adopt a facile method and highly active material to enhance conductive performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>. As one of the most promising conductive polymers,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0032_R_d9353325e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">32</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0038_R_d9353325e420" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the typical 1D hollow polypyrrole (PPy) nanotube has a large internal space, excellent flexibility, and stability, which can further enhance the intercalation/extraction kinetics of metal ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0037_R_d9353325e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochim. Acta">37</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0039_R_d9353325e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0043_R_d9353325e425" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Polymers">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The more electrochemical active sites can be fully exposed by increasing the interlayer spacing.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0044_R_d9353325e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Green Energy Environ.">44</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0050_R_d9353325e430" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, adopting an effective synthesis strategy to construct a MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite material with high conductivity, activity, and stability is of great significance for improving the electrochemical performance of LIBs and SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we presented a novel synthesis strategy to fabricate a MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy composite material for highly performance LIBs and SIBs. The synthesis diagram is shown in <b>Scheme</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0001">1</a>; the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy nanotubes were prepared by an efficient in situ solvothermal process. The 1D hollow PPy nanotube was used as substrate; sodium molybdate and thioacetamide serve as the molybdenum source and sulfur source, respectively. The as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy nanotubes exhibit many unique advantages. On the one hand, the 1D and 2D composite structure can improve both electrical conductivity and specific capacity. On another hand, the increased interlayer spacing of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> can facilitate the Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion and accelerate the mechanical strain of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy material during the charge and discharge progress. As a result of these merits, when used as anode materials, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy behaves splendid storage for lithium and sodium ions compared to pure MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/db125769-184f-4eaa-b0ac-13df417d81eb/ente202401010-cstr-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/db125769-184f-4eaa-b0ac-13df417d81eb/ente202401010-cstr-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/445a67a6-3277-430e-8db7-64a291d4f22e/ente202401010-cstr-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/db125769-184f-4eaa-b0ac-13df417d81eb/ente202401010-cstr-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Scheme 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202401010-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202401010-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ente202401010-sec-0003-title">2.1 Structural and Morphology Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The crystal structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is characterized using X-ray diffraction (XRD) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0002">1a</a>). The peak signal at 9.3° could be attributed to the (002) crystal plane of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>, which is consistent with the related reports.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0024_R_d9353325e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Appl. Catal., B">24</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e515" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The absence of other impurity peaks indicates that the prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy has higher purity. Figure S1, Supporting Information, exhibits the XRD pattern of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub>; the positions of characteristic peaks are agreement with that of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy. According to the Bragg equation (2<i>d</i>sin<i>θ </i>= <i>nλ</i>), the interlayer spacing for MoS<sub>2</sub> is 9.4 Å. This enlarged interlayer spacing may be caused by the guest substance generated during the solvothermal process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0035_R_d9353325e536" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As can be speculated, with the addition of PPy, the signal intensities of MoS<sub>2</sub> are unaffected.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2335b790-c2f6-4837-927c-cadd33ce6f45/ente202401010-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/2335b790-c2f6-4837-927c-cadd33ce6f45/ente202401010-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8547340d-8482-4210-b659-4077eb0598ed/ente202401010-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2335b790-c2f6-4837-927c-cadd33ce6f45/ente202401010-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy. b) FT-IR spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, PPy, and MoS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0002">1b</a> shows the Fourier transform infrared (FT-IR) spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, PPy, and MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively. The peak at 1549.6 cm<sup>−1</sup> corresponds to the stretching vibration mode of the pyrrole ring,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0032_R_d9353325e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Solid State Electrochem.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the peak at 1470.8 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be attributed to the asymmetric stretching vibration mode of C<span class="icomoon"></span>C bond, and the peak at 1041.5 cm<sup>−1</sup> relates to the in-plane vibration mode of C<span class="icomoon"></span>H bond, which further confirmed the existence of PPy.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0037_R_d9353325e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochim. Acta">37</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0049_R_d9353325e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Power Sources">49</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0050_R_d9353325e595" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectrum is used to further prove the composition and crystal structure of prepared materials and it also can distinguish different phase of the samples. Figure S2, Supporting Information, shows Raman peaks of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy heterostructures at 377 and 401 cm<sup>−1</sup>, which correspond to <i>E</i>1 2g (in-plane vibration of S and Mo atoms) and <i>A</i><sub>1g</sub> (out-plane vibration of S atoms in the counter direction) Mo–S phonon mode vibration of 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub>. The characteristic peaks at 1365 and 1579 cm<sup>−1</sup> are related to the <i>D</i> and <i>G</i> bands for PPy, which confirmed the existence of PPy. It should be pointed out that additional peaks appear at 148 (<i>J</i><sub>1</sub>), 225 (<i>J</i><sub>2</sub>), and 337 (<i>J</i><sub>3</sub>) cm<sup>−1</sup> are attributed to 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>. And the Raman peak at 285 cm<sup>−1</sup> is the <i>E</i><sub>1g</sub> (in-plane vibration of the S atom) mode vibration of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>.</p>
            
            <p>The fraction of MoS<sub>2</sub> in the structure of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is further measured by thermal gravimetric analysis (TGA) analysis. As shown in Figure S3, Supporting Information, the whole pyrolysis process is mainly divided into three steps in the air atmosphere. First, the weight loss is about 2.8% when the sample is heated to 212 °C, which are related to water vaporization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0050_R_d9353325e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Then, PPy began to thermal decomposition at temperature approaching 260 °C, which lost nearly 10.12%. Finally, when the temperature reaches ≈350 °C, some part of MoS<sub>2</sub> is slowly oxidized to MoO<sub>3</sub>; the following reaction equation is as follows: 2MoS<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> <b>→ </b>2MoO<sub>3</sub> + 4SO<sub>2</sub>. The proportion of MoS<sub>2</sub> oxidized can be calculated by relative molecular mass of MoS<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>3</sub> in the equation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0039_R_d9353325e675" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - J. Mater. Chem. A">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculated value (19.97%) and the proportion of MoS<sub>2</sub> that is not oxidized (67.13%) represent the proportion of MoS<sub>2</sub> in MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is 87.1%. These results further support the large-scale in situ synthesis of MoS<sub>2</sub> on PPy nanotubes.</p>
            
            <p>The morphology of the as-prepared materials is characterized via scanning electron microscope (SEM) and transmission electron microscope (TEM) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2</a>). As shown in Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2a</a>, the diameter of the synthesized PPy nanotube is ≈200 nm with a 1D hollow structure, and the thickness of the tube wall is ≈30 nm. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2b,c</a> is the SEM images of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy; it can be seen that the 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets vertically aligned on the surface of the hollow PPy nanotube. In addition, the lattice distribution in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy (Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2e–g</a>) shows that the MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets are contained by 1T and 2H phase. And the increased interlayer spacing can be also observed of 0.94 nm in HRTEM images (Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2g</a>), corresponding to the (002) crystal plane of 1T MoS<sub>2</sub>, which is consistent with the XRD results. The increased layer spacing is conducive to the rapid insertion and removal of lithium or sodium ions during charging and discharging progress.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0042_R_d9353325e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">42</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0043_R_d9353325e722" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Polymers">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the energy dispersive spectrometer (EDS) elemental mapping images (Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0003">2h,i</a>) of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy further demonstrate that the Mo, S, C, and N elements distribute relatively in the same region.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5282296e-0392-47dc-828f-7c0c2467a47a/ente202401010-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5282296e-0392-47dc-828f-7c0c2467a47a/ente202401010-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae2e6055-5a22-4494-be56-a0c247b2a687/ente202401010-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5282296e-0392-47dc-828f-7c0c2467a47a/ente202401010-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) SEM images of PPy, b,c) SEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, d–g) TEM and HRTEM images of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, and h,i) EDS elemental mapping of MoS<sub>2</sub>/Ppy.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The chemical states of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy are characterized by X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0004">3a</a> is the high-resolution XPS spectrum of Mo 3<i>d</i>; the binding energy of Mo 3<i>d</i> at 229.07 and 232.25 eV corresponds to Mo<sup>4+</sup> of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e782" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The binding energy of Mo 3<i>d</i> at 229.90 and 233.28 eV corresponds to Mo<sup>4+</sup> of 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0034_R_d9353325e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - J. Catal.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, the peak signal for S 2<i>p</i> can be assigned to 1T and 2H phases of MoS<sub>2</sub>. As shown in Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0004">3b</a>, the peaks found at ≈161.54 and 162.87 eV could be related to S 2<i>p</i><sub>3/2</sub> and S 2<i>p</i><sub>1/2</sub> of 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>, while the additional peaks at 162.31 and 163.72 eV of S 2<i>p</i> correspond to the 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0035_R_d9353325e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, inspired by many reported works,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0028_R_d9353325e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">28</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the percentage of 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> can be estimated (≈55%) by integrating these peaks areas.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0018_R_d9353325e833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Catal. Commun.">18</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0023_R_d9353325e833" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is further confirmed that the 1T and 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub> are almost equal proportion coexisted in MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy. The presence of highly conductive 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> can speed up the electron transport, while the 2H phase MoS<sub>2</sub> can further stabilize the structure of materials; this unique phase structure is favorable to mass transfer process when it is applied in LIBs or SIBs. Furthermore, in Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0004">3c</a>, three peaks placed at binding energies of 285, 285.9, and 288.4 eV, which is related to the C<span class="icomoon"></span>C, C<span class="icomoon"></span>N, and CO bonds, respectively. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0004">3d</a> shows the XPS spectrum of N 1<i>s</i>; the peak of N is very close to Mo 2<i>p</i><sub>2/3</sub>, so the obtained signal is weak. The binding energy of 399.97 and 397.07 eV can be attributed to pyrrole N and pyridine N derived from PPy,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0033_R_d9353325e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nanoscale Res. Lett.">33</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0046_R_d9353325e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - RSC Adv.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. The existence of PPy in the composite material mainly plays three roles: as a substrate for growing of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, further improve the conductivity of the composite materials, and provide rapid ion diffusion path due to 1D hollow structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0047_R_d9353325e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Eng. J.">47</a>-<a href="#ente202401010-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0050_R_d9353325e866" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Energy Storage Mater.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8402de0a-10a6-479d-a120-f0b72824a379/ente202401010-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/8402de0a-10a6-479d-a120-f0b72824a379/ente202401010-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1dd5a233-d709-4cea-8a21-0d944100d7d2/ente202401010-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8402de0a-10a6-479d-a120-f0b72824a379/ente202401010-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS spectra of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy: a) Mo 3<i>d</i>, b) S 2<i>p</i>, c) C 1<i>s</i>, and d) N 1<i>s</i>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="ente202401010-sec-0004-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0005">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="ente202401010-sec-0005-title">2.2.1 Electrochemical Performance of LIBs</h4>
               
               <p>The electrochemical performance of as-prepared materials is evaluated by utilizing MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy as anode materials for LIBs. Rate performance of LIBs is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0005">4a</a>; the values of specific capacity are 960.6, 742.2, 590.3, and 366.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 100, 300, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0005">4b</a> shows cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy for LIBs at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>; the specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy in the first discharge cycle is 1312.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and its capacity still remains 755.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. The capacity loss of the first cycle may be related to the irreversible structural changes of the interface (SEI) between the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and the electrolyte. During the charging process, the shuttle of lithium ions&nbsp;leaving a large number of Li vacancies, resulting in a thermodynamic unstable state of the electrode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0041_R_d9353325e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - J. Alloys Compd.">41</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0042_R_d9353325e951" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This situation also leads to the low coulomb efficiency of the first ten cycles for LIBs. By contrast, the specific capacity of pure MoS<sub>2</sub> in first discharge is 1155.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which decays fast after 150 cycles with only 331.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The specific capacity of pure PPy in first discharge is 72.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Figure S4, Supporting Information, shows the first three laps of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy measured at a specific current density at a voltage window of 0.01–3 V. The low coulomb efficiency of the first charge and discharge is due to the irreversible insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> in the MoS<sub>2</sub> lattice and the formation of the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) consumes a large amount of Li<sup>+</sup>. In the first discharge curve, two voltage platforms are observed around 1.1 and 0.6 V, of which the one at 1.1 V is attributed to the insertion of Li into the interlayer of MoS<sub>2</sub>, which is accompanied by a phase transition from 2H to 1T of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>. The platform at 0.5 V is related to the conversion reaction, in which Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> is decomposed in situ into Mo and Li<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0025_R_d9353325e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - New J. Chem.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode shows excellent rate performance and long-term stability. This is related to its stable structure provided by increased layer spacing 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> and hollow nanotube PPy.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/732b2a70-de78-4466-8811-2e4387e8827e/ente202401010-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/732b2a70-de78-4466-8811-2e4387e8827e/ente202401010-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ba37122-b171-4487-9781-c166584c7e84/ente202401010-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/732b2a70-de78-4466-8811-2e4387e8827e/ente202401010-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate performance and b) cycling performance at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy for LIBs.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>It can be seen from the statistical histogram of rate performance (Figure S5, Supporting Information), when the current density changed from 100 to 300, 500, and 1000 mAg<sup>−1</sup>, the average discharge ratio capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anodes is about 960.6, 742.2, 590.3, and 366.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively; while the current density returns to 100 mAg<sup>−1</sup>, the average discharge ratio capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anodes still maintains the high level of 863.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. However, the average specific capacity of pure MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes at the corresponding current density is 870.9, 687.7, 348.2, and 129.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to its initial value, its capacity decays to 351.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and shows a poor capacity retention rate. The pure PPy can provide an average of 65.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> capacity each lap during 100 charge and discharge processes. The well-defined structure guarantees excellent electrochemical performance. The increased layer spacing of 1T MoS<sub>2</sub> can provide much more internal space. Meanwhile, the vertical growth of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets on the surface of the PPy nanotube furnish active sites for Li ions and shorten the ion diffusion path to promote rapid ion transfer. In addition, the hollow interior allows the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode to effectively adapt to the strain caused by the huge volume change during the charge and discharge process, which could be beneficial for stability; this is confirmed by the SEM image of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy after electrochemical cycling (Figure S6, Supporting Information).</p>
               
               <p>The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0006">5a</a>. In the first cycle, the reduction peak at 0.5 V is attributed to the conversion of Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> to Li<sub>2</sub>S and Mo nanoparticles, and the formation of SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0036_R_d9353325e1080" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - J. Electroanal. Chem.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The other reduction peak at 1.4 V is the Li ions entering MoS<sub>2</sub> layers or defects to form the Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>. Two oxidation peaks at 1.8 and 2.3 V are attributed to the oxidation of the part of metal Mo to MoS<sub>2</sub> and the oxidation of Li<sub>2</sub>S to Li<sup>+</sup> and S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0029_R_d9353325e1099" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Energy Storage">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a87e25c3-288a-4ec5-8cce-85c1b5fbc59c/ente202401010-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/a87e25c3-288a-4ec5-8cce-85c1b5fbc59c/ente202401010-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4897ad69-a8e8-4bb7-ac10-ff4e0627d410/ente202401010-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a87e25c3-288a-4ec5-8cce-85c1b5fbc59c/ente202401010-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy for LIBs measured with a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> under a voltage window of 0.01–3 V. b) Nyquist plots of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and MoS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <p>From the last two scan onward, the reduction peak nearly 0.5 V is disappeared, and the two reduction peaks at 2.0 and 1.3 V correspond to the formation of Li<sub>2</sub>S and Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0037_R_d9353325e1146" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochim. Acta">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, little difference in the redox peaks can be observed for the second and third cycling curve, indicating an excellent reversibility of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode. Meanwhile, the capacity contribution in Li<sup>+</sup> storage is collected by CV curves (Figure S7, Supporting Information). It can be observed that the contribution of peudocapacitance steadily increases with increasing the scanning rates. The analysis result shows that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode exhibits considerable pseudocapacitance activity.</p>
               
               <p>In order to further investigate the reaction kinetics processes of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and MoS<sub>2</sub> anodes for LIBs, the electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) performance is evaluated and analyzed. As shown in Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0006">5b</a>, the Nyquist plots of two as-prepared anodes contain a sloping line and semicircle in the low- and high-frequency regions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0037_R_d9353325e1168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Electrochim. Acta">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The smaller diameter of the semicircle represents the lower charge transfer resistance, and it is more conducive to the rapid kinetic reaction. It can be seen that the charge transfer resistance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode is significantly lower than that of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub>, indicating that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy has faster charge transfer kinetics, which is due to the superior conductive performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy. The efficient electronic transmission is consistent with the existence of the metallic 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub>; moreover, the highly conductive 1D hollow PPy nanotube further promotes the improvement of reaction kinetics.</p>
               </section>
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0006">
               
               <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section3" id="ente202401010-sec-0006-title">2.2.2 Electrochemical Performance of SIBs</h4>
               
               <p>In order to further evaluate the superiority of this unique 1D/2D hybrid structure with enlarged interlayer spacing, the prepared samples were also utilized as the anode materials in SIBs for testing. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0007">6a</a> shows the rate capabilities of the cells at various current densities; the average discharge specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is 698.7, 519.6, 379.9, and 251.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the current density of 100, 300, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the specific capacity can still remain 626.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. The average discharge specific capacity of pure MoS<sub>2</sub> is 550.2, 425.2, 202.1, and 56.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at the same current density. When the current density returns to the initial value, its specific capacity only remains 270.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
               
               <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
                  <figure class="figure" id="ente202401010-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6cdab35f-1088-4bd0-bfdc-3f70ac7a97b8/ente202401010-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                           <source srcset="/cms/asset/6cdab35f-1088-4bd0-bfdc-3f70ac7a97b8/ente202401010-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3f66f550-ead2-4103-817f-920de1faf7a0/ente202401010-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6cdab35f-1088-4bd0-bfdc-3f70ac7a97b8/ente202401010-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                        <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=ente202401010-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fente.202401010" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                        </div>
                        <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy. b) Cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy for SIBs at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Statistical histogram of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy under different current density. d) Nyquist plots of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and MoS<sub>2</sub>.</div>
                     </figcaption>
                  </figure>
               </section>
               
               <div class="paragraph-element">Compared with Li<sup>+</sup>, the Na<sup>+</sup> has a larger radius, so the electrochemical reaction kinetics of insertion/extraction process will be more difficult. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0007">6b</a> shows the cycling performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy, MoS<sub>2</sub>, and PPy anodes at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode shows better cycling stability than pure MoS<sub>2</sub> and PPy. The initial discharge specific capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode is 975.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and it still has the good reversible specific capacity of 503.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. Meanwhile, the micromorphology of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy does not change significantly after electrochemical cycling process (Figure S8, Supporting Information). The initial discharge specific capacity of pure MoS<sub>2</sub> and PPy is 732.3 and 62.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. It can be seen that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy electrode exhibits excellent sodium storage capacity and cycling stability. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0007">6c</a> shows that the rate performance of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is significantly better than that of the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> and PPy. This is originating from two aspects. For one thing, its unique hollow 1D structure effectively shortens the diffusion path of sodium ions and the transmission distance of electrons. For another thing, the increased interlayer spacing is conducive to the rapid insertion/extraction of sodium ions, and also can effectively alleviate the volume change during the electrochemical cycling, so as to ensure the electrode material can still maintain its structure during the high current charge and discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0043_R_d9353325e1303" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Polymers">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the electrochemical performance of the material prepared in this study was compared with other MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite materials, as shown in Table S1, Supporting Information. It can be seen that the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy in this study delivers outstanding cycling stability and rate performance. Figure <a href="#ente202401010-fig-0007">6d</a> shows the Nyquist plots of pristine MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy shows a semicircle with a smaller diameter in the high-frequency region, which is mainly due to the introduction of conductive PPy nanotube. To further evaluate the sodium ion diffusion coefficient by linear fitting the low-frequency region of Nyquist plots (Figure S9, Supporting Information).
                  <div class="inline-equation" id="ente202401010-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE--><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5e036de3-a589-4dbc-938e-d89043f4f00e/ente202401010-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21944288:media:ente202401010:ente202401010-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/ente202401010-math-0001.png">

                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>D</mi>
                                 <mo>=</mo>
                                 <mfrac>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <msup>
                                          <mi>R</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </msup>
                                       <msup>
                                          <mi>T</mi>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                       </msup>
                                    </mrow>
                                    <mrow>
                                       <mn>2</mn>

                              </mrow>
                              <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$$D &amp;amp;#x00026;amp;amp;amp;amp;equals; \frac{R^{2} T^{2}}{2 A^{2} n^{4} F^{4} C^{2} \left(\sigma\right)^{2}}$$</annotation>
                           </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               </div>
               
               <p>According to the diffusion coefficient formula (Equation&nbsp;(<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#ente202401010-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a>)), the <i>R</i> represents gas constant (8.314 J mol<sup>−1</sup> K<sup>−1</sup>), <i>T</i> represents the room temperature (298.15 K), <i>A</i> represents the surface area of electrode, <i>n</i> represents the number of electrons, <i>F</i> represents Faraday constant (96 500 C mol<sup>−1</sup>), <i>C</i> is the initial concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> in the reaction electrode, and the <i>σ</i> is the Warburg coefficient obtained by linear fitting the low-frequency region of Nyquist plots.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#ente202401010-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0051_R_d9353325e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Nanotechnology">51</a>, <a href="#ente202401010-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="ente202401010-bib-0052_R_d9353325e1386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Appl. Mater. Interfaces">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The sodium ion diffusion coefficient of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy and MoS<sub>2</sub> is 1.42 × 10<sup>−13</sup> and 8.17 × 10<sup>−14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The rapid sodium ion diffusion kinetics is mainly due to the 1D hollow structure shortens the diffusion path of sodium ions and the increased interlayer spacing also provides more diffusion space.</p>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202401010-sec-0007">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202401010-sec-0007-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy is fabricated by an effective solvothermal method to combine the 1D hollow PPy nanotube and MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. The XRD, XPS, and TEM analyses reveal that the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy contains a composite structure with highly conductive 1T phase MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets and PPy nanotubes, which helps to facilitate ion exchange and hinder the volume expansion during&nbsp;charge–discharge processes for LIBs and SIBs. As a result, the performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode in LIBs and SIBs is superier to the pure MoS<sub>2</sub> anode in&nbsp;both rate performance and cyclic stability. The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy behaves lithium storage properties with stable capacity up to 755.5 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. As to SIBs, the MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy anode exhibits excellent performance with a high capacity of 503.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles. This study not only enhances the specific capacity of the MoS<sub>2</sub>-based anodes but also successfully sheds light on fabricating nanostructures toward MoS<sub>2</sub>-based composite material application.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="ente202401010-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202401010-sec-0008-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0009">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0010">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0011">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0011-title"> Reagents and Chemicals</h4>
                  
                  <p>Sodium molybdate (Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O, AR), thioacetamide (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>NS, AR), ethylene glycol (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, AR), ferric chloride (FeCl<sub>3</sub> 6H<sub>2</sub>O, AR), methyl orange (C<sub>14</sub>H<sub>14</sub>N<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>Na, AR), and pyrrole monomer were bought from Tianjin Kemiou Chemical Reagent Co., Ltd. and the deionized water was made by the equipment of Millpore Water System (18.2 MΩ cm).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0012">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0012-title"> Synthesis of Hollow PPy Nanotube</h4>
                  
                  <p>The typical preparation process of the hollow PPy nanotube is as follows: 0.8 g methyl orange and 6.5 g FeCl<sub>3</sub> 6H<sub>2</sub>O were dispersed in 480 mL deionized water with magnetic stirring for 10 min. Then, 0.67 g pyrrole monomer was added under stirring condition. The mixture was stood for 24 h in an ambient temperature of 4 °C, and then washed and centrifuged with ethanol and deionized water for 4 times, respectively. The product was obtained by freeze-drying process (LGJ-10) for 24 h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0013">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0013-title"> Synthesis of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy Composite Materials</h4>
                  
                  <p>The MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy composite materials were fabricated by a solvothermal method. Typically, 0.2 g Na<sub>2</sub>MoO<sub>4</sub>·2H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.6 g C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>NS were dispersed in a mixed solvent composited with 20 mL deionized water and 10 mL ethylene glycol. The as-prepared PPy nanotube was dispersed in the above solution and magnetic stirring for 30 min. Then, the solution was transferred into a 70 mL Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave and kept at 200 °C for 24 h. The sample was cooled to room temperature naturally, and then centrifuged and washed with deionized water and ethanol for 3 times, respectively. The final product (MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy) was obtained by freeze-drying (LGJ-10) process for 24 h. For comparison, pure MoS<sub>2</sub> was prepared without adding PPy nanotube and the other synthetic conditions were the same as preparation of MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0014">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0014-title"> Structural Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>XRD measurements were performed on D/max2200PC diffractometer with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 1.54178 Å). FT-IR spectra were recorded on the equipment of Bruker TENSOR27. XPS was conducted on ultrahigh vacuum VG Scientific Corp MK-II electron spectrometer with Al Kα radiation. The TGA was measured by the equipment of STA409PC from 30 to 600 °C in the air with ramp rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. The field emission SEM (Hitachi S-4800) and TEM (FEI Tecnai G2 F20) were used to study the microstructure and morphology of the samples.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0015">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0015-title"> Electrochemical Measurements: Preparation of the Working Electrodes</h4>
                  
                  <p>Electrochemical performance of the as-prepared materials was conducted by a half-cell LIBs and SIBs. The assembling process of coin cells (CR2032) was carried out according to the following steps. As-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub>/PPy composite nanotube and pure MoS<sub>2</sub> were used as electrode active materials, polyvinylidene fluoride was used as binder, and the acetylene black was used as conductive agent; the ratio of active materials, binder, and conductive agent was 8:1:1. <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone was added and the slurry was ground evenly in an agate mortar. Then, the slurry was coated on the copper foil, which was used as the current collector, and dried at 100 °C for 12 h in vacuum. The slurry-coated copper foil was cut into the slices with the diameter of 16 mm. The mass loading of the both of LIB and SIB electrodes is 0.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The coin cells were assembled in the Ar-filled glove box, lithium wafers were used as counter electrode and reference electrode, 1.0 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> lithium hexafluorophosphate (LiPF<sub>6</sub>) in a mixture of ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (EC/DMC) (1:1 in volume) as the electrolyte, and the Celgard 2400 polypropylene porous membrane was used as diaphragm. The assembly for SIBs is similar to that of LIBs; the difference is that the metallic sodium was used as the reference electrode and counter electrode, and the 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (1:1 in volume) was used as the electrolyte. The relevant components were assembled into coin cells in turn and let it stand for 24 h before electrochemical measurements.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="ente202401010-sec-0016">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="ente202401010-sec-0016-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>LAND (CT2001A) test system and Chenhua (CHI 660D) electrochemical workstation were used to measure the electrochemical performance of LIBs. The frequency range of EIS was 100 kHz–0.01 Hz; the initial voltage was the open-circuit voltage and the amplitude was 0.005 V. The CV measurements were performed on electrochemical workstation at the voltage range of 0.01–3.00 V (vs Li/Li<sup>+</sup>) with the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. The galvanostatic charge/discharge measurements were conducted at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, and the rate performance was carried out at a current density of 100, 300, 500, and 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The electrochemical measurements conditions of SIBs were similar to that of LIBs.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="ente202401010-sec-0017-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

                  <li data-bib-id="ente202401010-bib-0006"><span class="bullet">6</span>
                     <span class="author">G. Zubi</span>, <span class="author">R. Dufo-López</span>, <span class="author">M. Carvalho</span>, <span class="author">G.
